</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Food security</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Food_safety" title="Food safety">Food safety</a>.</div>
<p><b>Food security</b> is a condition related to the supply of food, and individuals' access to it. Concerns over food security have existed throughout history. There is evidence of <a href="/wiki/Granary" title="Granary">Granaries</a> being in use over 10,000 years ago, with central authorities in civilizations including <a href="/wiki/Ancient_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient China">ancient China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a> being known to release food from storage in times of famine. At the 1974 <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Conference" title="World Food Conference">World Food Conference</a> the term "food security" was defined with an emphasis on supply. Food security, they said, is the "availability at all times of adequate world food supplies of basic foodstuffs to sustain a steady expansion of food consumption and to offset fluctuations in production and prices".<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Later definitions added demand and access issues to the definition. The final report of the 1996 World Food Summit states that food security "exists when all people, at all times, have physical and economic access to sufficient, safe and nutritious food to meet their dietary needs and food preferences for an active and healthy life".<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FAO_1996_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_1996-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Household food security exists when all members, at all times, have access to enough food for an active, healthy life.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated8_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated8-4">[4]</a></sup> Individuals who are food secure do not live in <a href="/wiki/Hunger" title="Hunger">hunger</a> or fear of <a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">starvation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-5">[5]</a></sup> Food insecurity, on the other hand, is a situation of "limited or uncertain availability of nutritionally adequate and safe foods or limited or uncertain ability to acquire acceptable foods in socially acceptable ways", according to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">United States Department of Agriculture</a> (USDA).<sup id="cite_ref-Bickel_2000_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bickel_2000-6">[6]</a></sup> Food security incorporates a measure of resilience to future disruption or unavailability of critical food supply due to various risk factors including droughts, shipping disruptions, fuel shortages, economic instability, and wars.<sup id="cite_ref-Boeing2016_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing2016-7">[7]</a></sup> In the years 2011-2013, an estimated 842 million people were suffering from chronic hunger.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO.2C_WFP.2C_IFAD_2013_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO.2C_WFP.2C_IFAD_2013-8">[8]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> of the United Nations, or FAO, identified the four pillars of food security as availability, access, utilization, and stability.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2009_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2009-9">[9]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> (UN) recognized the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_Food" class="mw-redirect" title="Right to Food">Right to Food</a> in the Declaration of Human Rights in 1948,<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-5">[5]</a></sup> and has since noted that it is vital for the enjoyment of all other rights.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_1999_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_1999-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 1996 World Summit on Food Security declared that "food should not be used as an instrument for political and economic pressure".<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_1996_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_1996-3">[3]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Centre_for_Trade_and_Sustainable_Development" title="International Centre for Trade and Sustainable Development">International Centre for Trade and Sustainable Development</a>, failed agriculture market regulation and the lack of anti-dumping mechanisms cause much of the world's food scarcity and <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement">Measurement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Measurement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Food security indicators and measures are derived from country level household income and expenditure surveys to estimate per capita caloric availability.<sup id="cite_ref-Webb_et_al_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webb_et_al-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Perez-Escamilla_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Perez-Escamilla-12">[12]</a></sup> In general the objective of food security indicators and measures is to capture some or all of the main components of food security in terms of food availability, access and utilization or adequacy. While availability (production and supply) and utilization/adequacy (nutritional status/anthropometric measures) seemed much easier to estimate, thus more popular, access (ability to acquire sufficient quantity and quality) remain largely elusive.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrett_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett-13">[13]</a></sup> The factors influencing household food access are often context specific. Thus the financial and technical demands of collecting and analyzing data on all aspects of household's experience of food access and the development of valid and clear measures remain a huge challenge.<sup id="cite_ref-Swindale_.26_Bilinsky_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swindale_.26_Bilinsky-14">[14]</a></sup> Nevertheless, several measures have been developed that aim to capture the access component of food security, with some notable examples developed by the USAID-funded Food and Nutrition Technical Assistance (FANTA) project, collaborating with Cornell and Tufts University and Africare and World Vision.<sup id="cite_ref-Swindale_.26_Bilinsky_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swindale_.26_Bilinsky-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swindale.2C_A..2C_.26_Bilinsky.2C_P._15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swindale.2C_A..2C_.26_Bilinsky.2C_P.-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Coates_et_al_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coates_et_al-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ballard_et_al_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ballard_et_al-17">[17]</a></sup> These include:</p>
<ul>
<li><i>Household Food Insecurity Access Scale</i> (HFIAS) - continuous measure of the degree of food insecurity (access) in the household in the previous month</li>
<li><i>Household Dietary Diversity Scale</i> (HDDS) - measures the number of different food groups consumed over a specific reference period (24hrs/48hrs/7days).</li>
<li><i>Household Hunger Scale</i> (HHS)- measures the experience of household food deprivation based on a set of predictable reactions, captured through a survey and summarized in a scale.</li>
<li><i>Coping Strategies Index</i> (CSI) - assesses household behaviours and rates them based on a set of varied established behaviours on how households cope with food shortages. The methodology for this research is based on collecting data on a single question: "What do you do when you do not have enough food, and do not have enough money to buy food?"<sup id="cite_ref-Maxwell_1996_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maxwell_1996-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oldewage_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oldewage-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maxwell_et_al_2008_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maxwell_et_al_2008-20">[20]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Food insecurity is measured in the United States by questions in the Census Bureau's <a href="/wiki/Current_Population_Survey" title="Current Population Survey">Current Population Survey</a>. The questions asked are about anxiety that the household budget is inadequate to buy enough food, inadequacy in the quantity or quality of food eaten by adults and children in the household, and instances of reduced food intake or consequences of reduced food intake for adults and for children.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academy_of_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a> study commissioned by the USDA criticized this measurement and the relationship of "food security" to hunger, adding "it is not clear whether hunger is appropriately identified as the extreme end of the food security scale."<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/FAO" class="mw-redirect" title="FAO">FAO</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Programme" title="World Food Programme">World Food Programme</a> (WFP), and <a href="/wiki/International_Fund_for_Agricultural_Development" title="International Fund for Agricultural Development">International Fund for Agricultural Development</a> (IFAD) collaborate to produce <i>The State of Food Insecurity in the World</i>. The 2012 edition described improvements made by the FAO to the prevalence of undernourishment (PoU) indicator that is used to measure rates of food insecurity. New features include revised minimum dietary energy requirements for individual countries, updates to the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> data, and estimates of food losses in retail distribution for each country. Measurements that factor into the indicator include dietary energy supply, food production, food prices, food expenditures, and volatility of the <a href="/wiki/Food_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Food system">food system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO.2C_WFP.2C_IFAD_2012_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO.2C_WFP.2C_IFAD_2012-23">[23]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Famine_scales" title="Famine scales">stages of food insecurity</a> range from food secure situations to full-scale <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ayalew_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ayalew-24">[24]</a></sup> A new peer-reviewed journal of <i>Food Security: The Science, Sociology and Economics of Food Production and Access to Food</i> began publishing in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rates">Rates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Rates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FAO_Food_security_2012.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/FAO_Food_security_2012.jpg/220px-FAO_Food_security_2012.jpg" width="220" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/36/FAO_Food_security_2012.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="292" data-file-height="264" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FAO_Food_security_2012.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Number of people affected by undernourishment in 2010–12 (by region, in millions)<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With its prevalence of undernourishment (PoU) indicator, the FAO reported that almost 870 million people were chronically undernourished in the years 2010-2012. This represents 12.5% of the global population, or 1 in 8 people. Higher rates occur in developing countries, where 852 million people (about 15% of the population) are chronically undernourished. The report noted that Asia and Latin America have achieved reductions in rates of undernourishment that put these regions on track for achieving the Millennium Development Goal of halving the prevalence of undernourishment by 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO.2C_WFP.2C_IFAD_2012_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO.2C_WFP.2C_IFAD_2012-23">[23]</a></sup> The UN noted that about 2 billion people do not consume a sufficient amount of vitamins and minerals.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_World_Food_Day_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_World_Food_Day-27">[27]</a></sup> In India, the second-most populous country in the world, 30 million people have been added to the ranks of the hungry since the mid-1990s and 46% of children are <a href="/wiki/Underweight" title="Underweight">underweight</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Examples">Examples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Examples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Recentism" role="presentation">
<tr>

<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Recentism.svg" class="image"><img alt="Recentism.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Recentism.svg/46px-Recentism.svg.png" width="46" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Recentism.svg/69px-Recentism.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Recentism.svg/92px-Recentism.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="265" data-file-height="230" /></a></div>
</td>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jamestown_Colony">Jamestown Colony</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Jamestown Colony">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Due to a number of factors, the colony on <a href="/wiki/Jamestown_Island" title="Jamestown Island">Jamestown Island</a>, suffered a <a href="/wiki/Starving_Time" title="Starving Time">Starving Time</a> in the winter of 1609-1610 where approximately 80% of the settlers perished. There were about 500 Jamestown residents at the beginning of the winter. However, there were only 60 people still alive when the spring arrived.<sup id="cite_ref-wearestarved_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wearestarved-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Potato_Failure">European Potato Failure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: European Potato Failure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The European Potato Failure was a <a href="/wiki/Food_crisis" title="Food crisis">food crisis</a> caused by <a href="/wiki/Potato_blight" class="mw-redirect" title="Potato blight">potato blight</a> that struck Northern Europe in the mid-1840s. The time is also known as the Hungry Forties. While the crisis produced excess mortality and suffering across the affected areas, particularly affected were the Scottish Highlands and even more harshly Ireland. Many people starved due to lack of access to other staple food sources.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_2">World War 2</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: World War 2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The 872 days of <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Leningrad#Effect_on_the_city" title="Siege of Leningrad">The Siege of Leningrad</a> caused extreme famine in the Leningrad region through disruption of utilities, water, energy and food supplies. This resulted in the deaths of up to 1,500,000<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> soldiers and civilians and the evacuation of 1,400,000 more, mainly women and children, many of whom died during evacuation due to starvation and bombardment.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated8_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated8-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-31">[31]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dutch_famine_of_1944%E2%80%9345" title="Dutch famine of 1944–45">Dutch famine of 1944–45</a>, known as the <i>Hongerwinter</i> ("Hunger winter") in Dutch, was a famine that took place in the German-occupied part of the Netherlands, especially in the densely populated western provinces north of the great rivers, during the winter of 1944–45.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_versus_fuel">Food versus fuel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Food versus fuel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Food_versus_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Food versus fuel">Food versus fuel</a> is the dilemma regarding the risk of diverting farmland or crops for <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a> production to the detriment of the food supply.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2007-08_crisis">2007-08 crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 2007-08 crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In late 2007, export restrictions and panic buying, US dollar depreciation,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> increased farming for use in <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> world <a href="/wiki/Oil_prices" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil prices">oil prices</a> at more than $100 a barrel,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> global <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> loss of agricultural land to residential and industrial development,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> and growing consumer demand in China and India<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-39">[39]</a></sup> are claimed to have pushed up the price of grain.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> However, the role of some of these factors is under debate. Some argue the role of biofuel has been overplayed<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> as grain prices have come down to the levels of 2006. Nonetheless, <a href="/wiki/Food_riot" title="Food riot">food riots</a> took place in many countries across the world in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_security_in_the_US">Food security in the US</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Food security in the US">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Food_security_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Food security in the United States">Food security in the United States</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">United States Department of Agriculture</a> defines food insecurity as "limited or uncertain availability of nutritionally adequate and safe foods or limited or uncertain ability to acquire acceptable foods in socially acceptable ways."<sup id="cite_ref-Measurement_US_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Measurement_US-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<div class="floatright"><a href="/wiki/File:Food_Insecurity_by_Household.png" class="image" title="Food Insecurity by Household"><img alt="Food Insecurity by Household" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/52/Food_Insecurity_by_Household.png" width="450" height="570" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="570" /></a></div>
<p>National Food Security Surveys are the main survey tool used by the USDA to measure <a href="/wiki/Food_security_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Food security in the United States">food security in the United States</a>. Based on respondents' answers to survey questions, the household can be placed on a continuum of food security defined by the USDA. This continuum has four categories: high food security, marginal food security, low food security, and very low food security.<sup id="cite_ref-Measurement_US_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Measurement_US-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Economic_Research_Service" title="Economic Research Service">Economic Research Service</a> report number 155 (ERS-155) estimates that 14.5 percent (17.6 million) of US households were food insecure at some point in 2012. The prevalence of food insecurity has been relatively in the United States since the economic recession 2008.</p>
<p>In 2012:</p>
<ul>
<li>49.0 million people lived in food-insecure households.</li>
<li>12.4 million adults lived in households with very low food security.</li>
<li>8.3 million children lived in food-insecure households in which children, along with adults, were food insecure.</li>
<li>977,000 children lived in households in which one or more child experienced very low food security.<sup id="cite_ref-USDA-ERS.282012.29_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USDA-ERS.282012.29-46">[46]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feed_the_Future">Feed the Future</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Feed the Future">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The government of the United States has taken up an initiative along with other local government agencies as well as global partners, the G8 countries, and donors to reduce global hunger and to improve food security conditions in the world. Exploiting the G8 Summit of 2009 held in L'Aquila, Italy, President Barack Obama encouraged global leaders to revert the three-decade old trend of reduced agricultural investments, and instead, choose to increase cash flow in their respective agricultural sectors in a drive to bolster global food security. This led to the birth of the "<a href="/w/index.php?title=Feed_the_Future&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Feed the Future (page does not exist)">Feed the Future</a>" program.<sup id="cite_ref-the_Future_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-the_Future-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Initially, the U.S had been successful in collecting over $18 billion in funds for the program from the G8 countries as well as other donors. The "Feed the Future" initiative is presently led by the U.S Agency for International Development and has the support of other government-funded bodies like the State Department, Peace Corps, Millennium Challenge Corporation, the Treasury Department, U.S. Trade Representative, Overseas Private Investment Corporation, the U.S. African Development Foundation, and the U.S Department of Agriculture.</p>
<p>The target group of this initiative is the small holder farmers, especially women. Feed the Future has plans to help partner countries to develop their agricultural sector with an aim to improve their agricultural output. A spurt in economic growth would ultimately lead to higher income and help to eradicate hunger, and poverty, as well as under nutrition from the society. This initiative is expected to work on the basis of country-led priorities that call for consistent support by the governments, donor organizations, the private sector, and the civil society to accomplish its long-term goals.<sup id="cite_ref-the_Future_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-the_Future-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_Summit_on_Food_Security">World Summit on Food Security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: World Summit on Food Security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The World Summit on Food Security held in Rome in 1996, aimed to renew a global commitment to the fight against hunger. The Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations (FAO) called the summit in response to widespread under-nutrition and growing concern about the capacity of agriculture to meet future food needs. The conference produced two key documents, the Rome Declaration on World Food Security and the World Food Summit Plan of Action.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_1996_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_1996-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Rome Declaration calls for the members of the United Nations to work to halve the number of chronically undernourished people on the Earth by the year 2015. The Plan of Action sets a number of targets for government and non-governmental organizations for achieving food security, at the individual, household, national, regional and global levels.</p>
<p>Another World Summit on Food Security took place in Rome between November 16 and 18, 2009. The decision to convene the summit was taken by the Council of <a href="/wiki/FAO" class="mw-redirect" title="FAO">FAO</a> in June 2009, at the proposal of FAO Director-General Dr <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Diouf" title="Jacques Diouf">Jacques Diouf</a>. Heads of State and Government attended the summit, which took place at the FAO's headquarters.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pillars_of_food_security">Pillars of food security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Pillars of food security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The WHO states that there are three pillars that determine food security: food availability, food access, and food use.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The FAO adds a fourth pillar: the stability of the first three dimensions of food security over time.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-5">[5]</a></sup> In 2009, the World Summit on Food Security stated that the "four pillars of food security are availability, access, utilization, and stability".<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2009_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2009-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Availability">Availability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Availability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Food_production_per_capita.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Food_production_per_capita.svg/220px-Food_production_per_capita.svg.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Food_production_per_capita.svg/330px-Food_production_per_capita.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Food_production_per_capita.svg/440px-Food_production_per_capita.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Food_production_per_capita.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Growth in food production has been greater than population growth. Food per person increased since 1961. Data source: <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Food availability relates to the supply of food through production, distribution, and exchange.<sup id="cite_ref-Gregory_2005_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory_2005-50">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Food_production" class="mw-redirect" title="Food production">Food production</a> is determined by a variety of factors including <a href="/wiki/Land_ownership" class="mw-redirect" title="Land ownership">land ownership</a> and use; <a href="/wiki/Soil_management" title="Soil management">soil management</a>; crop selection, <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeding" title="Plant breeding">breeding</a>, and management; <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a> breeding and management; and <a href="/wiki/Harvesting" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvesting">harvesting</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_1997_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_1997-51">[51]</a></sup> Crop production can be affected by changes in rainfall and temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-Gregory_2005_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory_2005-50">[50]</a></sup> The use of land, water, and energy to grow food often competes with other uses, which can affect food production.<sup id="cite_ref-Godfray_2010_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Godfray_2010-52">[52]</a></sup> Land used for agriculture can be used for urbanization or lost to desertification, salinization, and soil erosion due to unsustainable agricultural practices.<sup id="cite_ref-Godfray_2010_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Godfray_2010-52">[52]</a></sup> Crop production is not required for a country to achieve food security. Nations don't have to have the natural resources required to produce crops in order to achieve food security, as seen in the examples of Japan<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and Singapore.<sup id="cite_ref-Tweeten_1999_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tweeten_1999-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because food consumers outnumber producers in every country,<sup id="cite_ref-Tweeten_1999_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tweeten_1999-54">[54]</a></sup> food must be distributed to different regions or nations. <a href="/wiki/Food_distribution" title="Food distribution">Food distribution</a> involves the storage, processing, transport, packaging, and marketing of food.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_1997_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_1997-51">[51]</a></sup> Food-chain infrastructure and storage technologies on farms can also affect the amount of food wasted in the distribution process.<sup id="cite_ref-Godfray_2010_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Godfray_2010-52">[52]</a></sup> Poor transport infrastructure can increase the price of supplying water and fertilizer as well as the price of moving food to national and global markets.<sup id="cite_ref-Godfray_2010_52-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Godfray_2010-52">[52]</a></sup> Around the world, few individuals or households are continuously self-reliant for food. This creates the need for a bartering, exchange, or cash economy to acquire food.<sup id="cite_ref-Gregory_2005_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory_2005-50">[50]</a></sup> The exchange of food requires efficient trading systems and market institutions, which can affect food security.<sup id="cite_ref-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012-55">[55]</a></sup> Per capita world food supplies are more than adequate to provide food security to all, and thus food accessibility is a greater barrier to achieving food security.<sup id="cite_ref-Tweeten_1999_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tweeten_1999-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Access">Access</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Access">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Goats_are_an_important_part_of_the_solution_to_global_food_security_because_they_are_fairly_low_maintenance_and_easy_to_raise_and_farm.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Goats_are_an_important_part_of_the_solution_to_global_food_security_because_they_are_fairly_low_maintenance_and_easy_to_raise_and_farm.jpg/220px-Goats_are_an_important_part_of_the_solution_to_global_food_security_because_they_are_fairly_low_maintenance_and_easy_to_raise_and_farm.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Goats_are_an_important_part_of_the_solution_to_global_food_security_because_they_are_fairly_low_maintenance_and_easy_to_raise_and_farm.jpg/330px-Goats_are_an_important_part_of_the_solution_to_global_food_security_because_they_are_fairly_low_maintenance_and_easy_to_raise_and_farm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Goats_are_an_important_part_of_the_solution_to_global_food_security_because_they_are_fairly_low_maintenance_and_easy_to_raise_and_farm.jpg/440px-Goats_are_an_important_part_of_the_solution_to_global_food_security_because_they_are_fairly_low_maintenance_and_easy_to_raise_and_farm.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Goats_are_an_important_part_of_the_solution_to_global_food_security_because_they_are_fairly_low_maintenance_and_easy_to_raise_and_farm.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Goats are an important part of the solution to global food security because they are fairly low-maintenance and easy to raise and farm.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Food access refers to the affordability and allocation of food, as well as the preferences of individuals and households.<sup id="cite_ref-Gregory_2005_50-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory_2005-50">[50]</a></sup> The UN Committee on Economic, Social, and Cultural Rights noted that the causes of <a href="/wiki/Hunger" title="Hunger">hunger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> are often not a scarcity of food but an inability to access available food, usually due to <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_1999_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_1999-10">[10]</a></sup> Poverty can limit access to food, and can also increase how vulnerable an individual or household is to food price spikes.<sup id="cite_ref-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012-55">[55]</a></sup> Access depends on whether the household has enough income to purchase food at prevailing prices or has sufficient land and other resources to grow its own food.<sup id="cite_ref-Garrett_and_Ruel_1999_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garrett_and_Ruel_1999-56">[56]</a></sup> Households with enough resources can overcome <a href="/wiki/Harvest_failure" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvest failure">unstable harvests</a> and <a href="/wiki/Local_food" title="Local food">local food</a> shortages and maintain their access to food.<sup id="cite_ref-Tweeten_1999_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tweeten_1999-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are two distinct types of access to food: direct access, in which a household produces food using human and material resources, and economic access, in which a household purchases food produced elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_1997_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_1997-51">[51]</a></sup> Location can affect access to food and which type of access a family will rely on.<sup id="cite_ref-Garrett_and_Ruel_1999_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garrett_and_Ruel_1999-56">[56]</a></sup> The assets of a household, including income, land, products of labor, inheritances, and gifts can determine a household's access to food.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_1997_51-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_1997-51">[51]</a></sup> However, the ability to access to sufficient food may not lead to the purchase of food over other materials and services.<sup id="cite_ref-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012-55">[55]</a></sup> Demographics and education levels of members of the household as well as the gender of the household head determine the preferences of the household, which influences the type of food that are purchased.<sup id="cite_ref-Garrett_and_Ruel_1999_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garrett_and_Ruel_1999-56">[56]</a></sup> A household's access to enough and nutritious food may not assure adequate food intake of all household members, as intrahousehold food allocation may not sufficiently meet the requirements of each member of the household.<sup id="cite_ref-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012-55">[55]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/USDA" class="mw-redirect" title="USDA">USDA</a> adds that access to food must be available in socially acceptable ways, without, for example, resorting to emergency food supplies, scavenging, stealing, or other coping strategies.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated8_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated8-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Utilization">Utilization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Utilization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The next pillar of food security is food utilization, which refers to the metabolism of food by individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-Tweeten_1999_54-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tweeten_1999-54">[54]</a></sup> Once food is obtained by a household, a variety of factors affect the quantity and quality of food that reaches members of the household. In order to achieve food security, the food ingested must be safe and must be enough to meet the physiological requirements of each individual.<sup id="cite_ref-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012_55-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012-55">[55]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Food_safety" title="Food safety">Food safety</a> affects food utilization,<sup id="cite_ref-Gregory_2005_50-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory_2005-50">[50]</a></sup> and can be affected by the preparation, processing, and cooking of food in the community and household.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_1997_51-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_1997-51">[51]</a></sup> Nutritional values<sup id="cite_ref-Gregory_2005_50-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory_2005-50">[50]</a></sup> of the household determine <a href="/wiki/Food_choice" title="Food choice">food choice</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_1997_51-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_1997-51">[51]</a></sup> and whether food meets cultural preferences is important to utilization in terms of psychological and social <a href="/wiki/Well-being" title="Well-being">well-being</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lorger_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lorger-57">[57]</a></sup> Access to healthcare is another determinant of food utilization, since the health of individuals controls how the food is metabolized.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_1997_51-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_1997-51">[51]</a></sup> For example, intestinal parasites can take nutrients from the body and decrease food utilization.<sup id="cite_ref-Tweeten_1999_54-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tweeten_1999-54">[54]</a></sup> Sanitation can also decrease the occurrence and spread of diseases that can affect food utilization.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_1997_51-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_1997-51">[51]</a></sup> Education about nutrition and food preparation can affect food utilization and improve this pillar of food security.<sup id="cite_ref-Tweeten_1999_54-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tweeten_1999-54">[54]</a></sup> education bring accountability among people,create awareness,which foster them for food security</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stability">Stability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Stability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Food stability refers to the ability to obtain food over time. Food insecurity can be transitory, seasonal, or chronic.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_1997_51-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_1997-51">[51]</a></sup> In transitory food insecurity, food may be unavailable during certain periods of time.<sup id="cite_ref-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012_55-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012-55">[55]</a></sup> At the food production level, <a href="/wiki/Natural_disasters" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural disasters">natural disasters</a><sup id="cite_ref-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012_55-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012-55">[55]</a></sup> and drought<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_1997_51-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_1997-51">[51]</a></sup> result in crop failure and decreased food availability. Civil conflicts can also decrease access to food.<sup id="cite_ref-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012_55-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012-55">[55]</a></sup> Instability in markets resulting in food-price spikes can cause transitory food insecurity. Other factors that can temporarily cause food insecurity are loss of employment or productivity, which can be caused by illness. Seasonal food insecurity can result from the regular pattern of growing seasons in food production.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_1997_51-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_1997-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Chronic (or permanent) food insecurity is defined as the long-term, persistent lack of adequate food.<sup id="cite_ref-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012_55-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ecker_and_Breisinger_2012-55">[55]</a></sup> In this case, households are constantly at risk of being unable to acquire food to meet the needs of all members. Chronic and transitory food insecurity are linked, since the reoccurrence of transitory food security can make households more vulnerable to chronic food insecurity.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_1997_51-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_1997-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_food_insecurity">Effects of food insecurity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Effects of food insecurity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>"Famine and hunger are both rooted in food insecurity. Chronic food insecurity translates into a high degree of vulnerability to famine and hunger; ensuring food security presupposes elimination of that vulnerability."<sup id="cite_ref-Ayalew_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ayalew-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stunting_and_chronic_nutritional_deficiencies">Stunting and chronic nutritional deficiencies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Stunting and chronic nutritional deficiencies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">Malnutrition</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kwashiorkor_6903.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Kwashiorkor_6903.jpg/220px-Kwashiorkor_6903.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Kwashiorkor_6903.jpg/330px-Kwashiorkor_6903.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Kwashiorkor_6903.jpg/440px-Kwashiorkor_6903.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1642" data-file-height="1226" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kwashiorkor_6903.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Children with symptoms of low calorie and protein intake and a nurse attendant at a Nigerian orphanage in the late 1960s.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Many countries experience ongoing food shortages and distribution problems. These result in chronic and often widespread hunger amongst significant numbers of people. <a href="/wiki/Human_population" class="mw-redirect" title="Human population">Human populations</a> can respond to chronic <a href="/wiki/Hunger" title="Hunger">hunger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> by decreasing body size, known in medical terms as <a href="/wiki/Stunted_growth" title="Stunted growth">stunting</a> or stunted growth. This process starts <i>in utero</i> if the mother is malnourished and continues through approximately the third year of life. It leads to higher infant and child mortality, but at rates far lower than during famines. Once stunting has occurred, improved nutritional intake after the age of about two years is unable to reverse the damage. Stunting itself can be viewed as a coping mechanism, bringing body size into alignment with the calories available during adulthood in the location where the child is born. Limiting body size as a way of adapting to low levels of energy (calories) adversely affects health in three ways:</p>
<ul>
<li>Premature failure of vital organs during adulthood. For example, a 50-year-old individual might die of heart failure because his/her heart suffered structural defects during early development;</li>
<li>Stunted individuals suffer a higher rate of disease and illness than those who have not undergone stunting;</li>
<li>Severe malnutrition in early childhood often leads to defects in cognitive development.<sup id="cite_ref-escape_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-escape-58">[58]</a></sup> It therefore creates disparity among children who did not experience severe malnutrition and those who experience it.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Challenges_to_achieving_food_security">Challenges to achieving food security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Challenges to achieving food security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_water_crisis">Global water crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Global water crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Water_resource_policy" title="Water resource policy">Water resource policy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vallee_fertile_du_Nil_a_Louxor.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Vallee_fertile_du_Nil_a_Louxor.jpg/220px-Vallee_fertile_du_Nil_a_Louxor.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Vallee_fertile_du_Nil_a_Louxor.jpg/330px-Vallee_fertile_du_Nil_a_Louxor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Vallee_fertile_du_Nil_a_Louxor.jpg/440px-Vallee_fertile_du_Nil_a_Louxor.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2080" data-file-height="1368" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vallee_fertile_du_Nil_a_Louxor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">Irrigation</a> canals have opened dry desert areas of Egypt to agriculture.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Water_deficits" class="mw-redirect" title="Water deficits">Water deficits</a>, which are already spurring heavy grain imports in numerous smaller countries,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> may soon do the same in larger countries, such as China or India.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Water_table" title="Water table">water tables</a> are falling in scores of countries (including northern China, the US, and India) due to widespread <a href="/wiki/Overdrafting" title="Overdrafting">overpumping</a> using powerful diesel and electric pumps. Other countries affected include Pakistan, Afghanistan, and Iran. This will eventually lead to <a href="/wiki/Water_scarcity" title="Water scarcity">water scarcity</a> and cutbacks in grain harvest. Even with the overpumping of its <a href="/wiki/Aquifers" class="mw-redirect" title="Aquifers">aquifers</a>, China is developing a grain deficit.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> When this happens, it will almost certainly drive grain prices upward. Most of the 3 billion people projected to be born worldwide by mid-century will be born in countries already experiencing <a href="/wiki/Water_shortages" class="mw-redirect" title="Water shortages">water shortages</a>. After China and India, there is a second tier of smaller countries with large water deficits – Afghanistan, Algeria, Egypt, Iran, Mexico, and Pakistan. Four of these already import a large share of their grain. Only Pakistan remains self-sufficient. But with a population expanding by 4 million a year, it will likely soon turn to the world market for grain.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Regionally, <a href="/wiki/Water_scarcity_in_Africa" title="Water scarcity in Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a> has the largest number of water-stressed countries of any place on the globe, as of an estimated 800 million people who live in Africa, 300 million live in a water-stressed environment.<sup id="cite_ref-IaC_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IaC-64">[64]</a></sup> It is estimated that by 2030, 75 million to 250 million people in <a href="/wiki/Water_scarcity_in_Africa" title="Water scarcity in Africa">Africa</a> will be living in areas of high water stress, which will likely displace anywhere between 24 million and 700 million people as conditions become increasingly unlivable.<sup id="cite_ref-IaC_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IaC-64">[64]</a></sup> Because the majority of Africa remains dependent on an agricultural lifestyle and 80 to 90 percent of all families in rural Africa rely upon producing their own food,<sup id="cite_ref-WWD_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WWD-65">[65]</a></sup> water scarcity translates to a loss of food security.</p>
<p>Multimillion-dollar investments beginning in the 1990s by the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> have reclaimed <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert</a> and turned the <a href="/wiki/Ica,_Peru" title="Ica, Peru">Ica</a> Valley in Peru, one of the driest places on earth, into the largest supplier of <a href="/wiki/Asparagus" title="Asparagus">asparagus</a> in the world. However, the constant irrigation has caused a rapid drop in the water table, in some places as much as eight meters per year, one of the fastest rates of aquifer depletion in the world. The wells of small farmers and local people are beginning to run dry and the water supply for the main city in the valley is under threat. As a cash crop, asparagus has provided jobs for local people, but most of the money goes to the buyers, mainly the British. A 2010 report concluded that the industry is not sustainable and accuses investors, including the World Bank, of failing to take proper responsibility for the effect of their decisions on the water resources of poorer countries.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Diverting water from the headwaters of the <a href="/wiki/Ica_River" title="Ica River">Ica</a> River to asparagus fields has also led to a water shortage in the mountain region of <a href="/wiki/Huancavelica" title="Huancavelica">Huancavelica</a>, where indigenous communities make a marginal living herding.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian.co.uk_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian.co.uk-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_degradation">Land degradation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Land degradation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Land_degradation" title="Land degradation">Land degradation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">Desertification</a></div>
<p>Intensive farming often leads to a vicious cycle of exhaustion of soil fertility and decline of agricultural yields.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Approximately 40 percent of the world's agricultural land is seriously degraded.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> In Africa, if current trends of soil degradation continue, the continent might be able to feed just 25 percent of its population by 2025, according to <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_University" title="United Nations University">UNU</a>'s Ghana-based Institute for Natural Resources in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_agriculture" title="Climate change and agriculture">Climate change and agriculture</a></div>
<p>Extreme events, such as droughts and floods, are forecast to increase as climate change and <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> takes hold.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Ranging from overnight floods to gradually worsening droughts, these will have a range of effects on the agricultural sector. By 2040, almost the entire Nile region, which once included large areas of irrigated agricultural land, is expected to become hot desert where cultivation is impossible due to water limitation.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Climate_%26_Development_Knowledge_Network" class="mw-redirect" title="Climate &amp; Development Knowledge Network">Climate &amp; Development Knowledge Network</a> report <i>Managing Climate Extremes and Disasters in the Agriculture Sectors: Lessons from the IPCC SREX Report</i>, the effects will include changing productivity and livelihood patterns, economic losses, and effects on infrastructure, markets and food security. Food security in future will be linked to our ability to adapt agricultural systems to extreme events. For example, the Garifuna women in <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a> are helping to ensure food security locally by reviving and improving production of traditional root crops, building up traditional methods of soil conservation, carrying out training in organic composting and pesticide use and creating the first Garifuna farmers' market. Sixteen towns have worked together to establish tool and seed banks. Efforts to plant wild fruit trees along the coast are helping to prevent <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">soil erosion</a>. The aim is to reduce the communities' vulnerability to the hazards of shifting weather patterns.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Approximately 2.4 billion people live in the <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a> of the Himalayan rivers.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> India, China, Pakistan, Afghanistan, Bangladesh, Nepal and <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> could experience floods followed by severe droughts in coming decades.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> In India alone, the Ganges provides water for drinking and farming for more than 500 million people.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The west coast of North America, which gets much of its water from glaciers in mountain ranges such as the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Nevada_(U.S.)" title="Sierra Nevada (U.S.)">Sierra Nevada</a>, also would be affected.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Glaciers aren't the only worry that the developing nations have; <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">sea level</a> is reported to rise as climate change progresses, reducing the amount of land available for agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>In other parts of the world, a big effect will be low yields of grain according to the World Food Trade Model, specifically in the low latitude regions where much of the developing world is located. From this the price of grain will rise, along with the developing nations trying to grow the grain. Due to this, every 2–2.5% price hike will increase the number of hungry people by 1%.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Low crop yields are just one of the problem facing farmers in the low latitudes and tropical regions. The timing and length of the growing seasons, when farmers plant their crops, are going to be changing dramatically, per the USDA, due to unknown changes in soil temperature and moisture conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another way of thinking about food security and <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> comes from Evan Fraser, a geographer working at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Guelph" title="University of Guelph">University of Guelph</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a> <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>. His approach is to explore the vulnerability of <a href="/wiki/Food_systems" title="Food systems">food systems</a> to climate change and he defines vulnerability to climate change as situations that occur when relatively minor environmental problems cause major effects on food security. Examples of this include the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Potato_Famine" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Potato Famine">Irish Potato Famine</a><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (November 2013)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Food_security#Dubious" title="Talk:Food security">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup>, which was caused by a rainy year that created ideal conditions for the fungal blight to spread in potato fields, or the <a href="/wiki/1983-1985_famine_in_Ethiopia" class="mw-redirect" title="1983-1985 famine in Ethiopia">Ethiopian Famine in the early 1980s</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-83">[83]</a></sup> Three factors stand out as common in such cases, and these three factors act as a diagnostic "tool kit" through which to identify cases where food security may be vulnerable to climate change. These factors are: (1) specialized <a href="/wiki/Agroecosystems" class="mw-redirect" title="Agroecosystems">agro-ecosystems</a>; (2) households with very few livelihood options other than farming; (3) situations where formal institutions do not provide adequate safety nets to protect people.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-83">[83]</a></sup> "The International Food Policy Research Institute (IFPRI) estimates that an additional US$ 7.1-7.3 billion per year are needed in agricultural investments to offset the negative <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">effect of climate change</a> on nutrition for children by 2050 (Table 6)."<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p><br /></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_diseases">Agricultural diseases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Agricultural diseases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stripe_rust.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Stripe_rust.jpg/220px-Stripe_rust.jpg" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Stripe_rust.jpg/330px-Stripe_rust.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Stripe_rust.jpg/440px-Stripe_rust.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="409" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stripe_rust.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Stripe rust on a wheat stem</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Diseases affecting livestock or crops can have devastating effects on food availability especially if there are no contingency plans in place. For example, <a href="/wiki/Ug99" title="Ug99">Ug99</a>, a lineage of wheat <a href="/wiki/Stem_rust" title="Stem rust">stem rust</a> which can cause up to 100% crop losses, is present in wheat fields in several countries in Africa and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and is predicted to spread rapidly through these regions and possibly further afield, potentially causing a wheat production disaster that would affect food security worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewSci_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewSci-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Crop_wild_relatives" class="mw-redirect" title="Crop wild relatives">crop wild relatives</a> of wheat can be used to improve modern varieties to be more resistant to rust. In their <a href="/wiki/Centers_of_origin" class="mw-redirect" title="Centers of origin">centers of origin</a> wild wheat plants are screened for resistance to rust, then their genetic information is studied and finally wild plants and modern varieties are crossed through means of modern <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeding" title="Plant breeding">plant breeding</a> in order to transfer the resistance genes from the wild plants to the modern varieties.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dictatorship_and_kleptocracy">Dictatorship and kleptocracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Dictatorship and kleptocracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Political_corruption" title="Political corruption">Political corruption</a></div>
<p>Nobel Prize winning economist <a href="/wiki/Amartya_Sen" title="Amartya Sen">Amartya Sen</a> has observed that "there is no such thing as an apolitical food problem." While drought and other naturally occurring events may trigger famine conditions, it is government action or inaction that determines its severity, and often even whether or not a famine will occur. The 20th century has examples of governments, such as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Leap_Forward" title="Great Leap Forward">Great Leap Forward</a> in the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (October 2014)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> undermining the food security of their own nations – sometimes intentionally, such as the <a href="/wiki/Hunger_Plan" title="Hunger Plan">Hunger Plan</a> enacted by <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.</p>
<p>When governments come to power by force or rigged elections, and not by way of fair and open elections, their base of support is often narrow and built upon <a href="/wiki/Cronyism" title="Cronyism">cronyism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patronage" title="Patronage">patronage</a>. Under such conditions "The distribution of food within a country is a political issue. Governments in most countries give priority to urban areas, since that is where the most influential and powerful families and enterprises are usually located. The government often neglects subsistence farmers and rural areas in general. The more remote and underdeveloped the area the less likely the government will be to effectively meet its needs. Many agrarian policies, especially the pricing of agricultural commodities, discriminate against rural areas. Governments often keep prices of basic grains at such artificially low levels that subsistence producers cannot accumulate enough capital to make investments to improve their production. Thus, they are effectively prevented from getting out of their precarious situation."<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Further dictators and <a href="/wiki/Warlord" title="Warlord">warlords</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (October 2014)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> have used food as a political weapon, rewarding their supporters while denying food supplies to areas that oppose their rule. Under such conditions food becomes a currency with which to buy support and famine becomes an effective weapon to be used against the opposition.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (October 2014)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Governments<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (October 2014)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> with strong tendencies towards <a href="/wiki/Kleptocracy" title="Kleptocracy">kleptocracy</a> can undermine food security even when harvests are good. When government monopolizes trade, farmers may find that they are free to grow cash crops for export, but under penalty of law only able to sell their crops to government buyers at prices far below the world market price.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (October 2014)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup> The government then is free to sell their crop on the world market at full price, pocketing the difference. This creates an artificial "<a href="/wiki/Poverty_trap" title="Poverty trap">poverty trap</a>" from which even the most hard working and motivated farmers may not escape.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (October 2014)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>When the <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a> is absent, or <a href="/wiki/Private_property" title="Private property">private property</a> is non-existent, farmers have little incentive to improve their productivity.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (October 2014)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> If a farm becomes noticeably more productive than neighboring farms, it may become the target of individuals well connected to the government. Rather than risk being noticed and possibly losing their land, farmers may be content with the perceived safety of mediocrity.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>As pointed out by <a href="/wiki/William_Bernstein" class="mw-redirect" title="William Bernstein">William Bernstein</a> in his book <i>The Birth of Plenty</i>: "Individuals without property are susceptible to starvation, and it is much easier to bend the fearful and hungry to the will of the state. If a [farmer's] property can be arbitrarily threatened by the state, that power will inevitably be employed to intimidate those with divergent political and religious opinions."</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_sovereignty">Food sovereignty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Food sovereignty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The approach known as <a href="/wiki/Food_sovereignty" title="Food sovereignty">food sovereignty</a> views the business practices of multinational corporations as a form of <a href="/wiki/Neocolonialism" title="Neocolonialism">neocolonialism</a>. It contends that multinational corporations have the financial resources available to buy up the agricultural resources of impoverished nations, particularly in the tropics. They also have the political clout to convert these resources to the exclusive production of <a href="/wiki/Cash_crops" class="mw-redirect" title="Cash crops">cash crops</a> for sale to <a href="/wiki/Industrialized_nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrialized nations">industrialized nations</a> outside of the tropics, and in the process to squeeze the poor off of the more productive lands.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian.co.uk_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian.co.uk-67">[67]</a></sup> Under this view <a href="/wiki/Subsistence_farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Subsistence farming">subsistence farmers</a> are left to cultivate only lands that are so marginal in terms of productivity as to be of no interest to the multinational corporations. Likewise, food sovereignty holds it to be true that communities should be able to define their own means of production and that food is a basic human right. With several multinational corporations now pushing agricultural technologies on developing countries, technologies that include improved seeds, chemical fertilizers, and pesticides, crop production has become an increasingly analyzed and debated issue. Many communities calling for food sovereignty are protesting the imposition of Western technologies on to their indigenous systems and agency.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Risks_to_food_security">Risks to food security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Risks to food security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%932008_world_food_price_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2007–2008 world food price crisis">2007–2008 world food price crisis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Food_prices" title="Food prices">Food prices</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_growth">Population growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Population growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">World population</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_(bad_effects).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg/220px-Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg/330px-Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg/440px-Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2780" data-file-height="2276" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_(bad_effects).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">family planning</a> placard in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>. It shows some negative effects of having too many children.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Current UN projections show a continued increase in population in the near future (but a steady decline in the population growth rate), with the global population expected to reach between 8.3 and 10.9 billion by 2050. UN Population Division estimates for the year 2150 range between 3.2 and 24.8 billion; mathematical modeling supports the lower estimate. Some analysts have questioned the sustainability of further world population growth, highlighting the growing pressures on the environment, global food supplies, and energy resources. Solutions for feeding the nine billion in the future are being studied and documented.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> One out of every seven people on our planet go to sleep hungry. People are suffering due to <a href="/wiki/Overpopulation" title="Overpopulation">overpopulation</a>, 25,000 people die of malnutrition and hunger related diseases everyday.</p>
