<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_fuel_dependence">Fossil fuel dependence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Fossil fuel dependence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture#Agriculture_and_petroleum" title="Agriculture">Agriculture and petroleum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil#Agricultural_effects_and_population_limits" title="Peak oil">Peak oil's effects on agriculture</a></div>
<p>While agricultural output increased as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a>, the energy input into the process (that is, the energy that must be expended to produce a crop) has also increased at a greater rate, so that the ratio of crops produced to energy input has decreased over time. Green Revolution techniques also heavily rely on chemical <a href="/wiki/Fertilizers" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertilizers">fertilizers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pesticides" class="mw-redirect" title="Pesticides">pesticides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herbicides" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbicides">herbicides</a>, some of which must be developed from fossil fuels, making agriculture increasingly reliant on petroleum products.</p>
<p>Between 1950 and 1984, as the Green Revolution transformed agriculture around the globe, world grain production increased by 250%. The energy for the Green Revolution was provided by <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Fertilizers" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertilizers">fertilizers</a> (natural gas), <a href="/wiki/Pesticides" class="mw-redirect" title="Pesticides">pesticides</a> (oil), and <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> fueled <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>David Pimentel, professor of ecology and agriculture at <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a>, and Mario Giampietro, senior researcher at the National Research Institute on Food and Nutrition (NRIFN), place in their study <i>Food, Land, Population and the U.S. Economy</i> the maximum <a href="/wiki/U.S._population" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. population">U.S. population</a> for a <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainable economy</a> at 210 million. To achieve a sustainable economy and avert disaster, the United States must reduce its population by at least one-third, and world population will have to be reduced by two-thirds, says the study.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>The authors of this study believe that the mentioned agricultural crisis will only begin to affect us after 2020, and will not become critical until 2050. The oncoming <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peaking of global oil</a> production (and subsequent decline of production), along with the peak of North American natural gas production will very likely precipitate this agricultural crisis much sooner than expected.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-39">[39]</a></sup> Geologist <a href="/wiki/Dale_Allen_Pfeiffer" title="Dale Allen Pfeiffer">Dale Allen Pfeiffer</a> claims that coming decades could see spiraling food prices without relief and massive <a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">starvation</a> on a global level such as never experienced before.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Homogeneity_in_the_global_food_supply">Homogeneity in the global food supply</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Homogeneity in the global food supply">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Glycine_max_003.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Glycine_max_003.JPG/220px-Glycine_max_003.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Glycine_max_003.JPG/330px-Glycine_max_003.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Glycine_max_003.JPG/440px-Glycine_max_003.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1722" data-file-height="1292" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Glycine_max_003.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A small number of major crops, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">Soybean</a>, have formed an increasing share of the food energy, protein, fat, and food weight eaten by the world's population over the past 50 years<sup id="cite_ref-Khoury_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khoury-94">[94]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since 1961, human diets across the world have become more diverse in the consumption of major commodity staple crops, with a corollary decline in consumption of local or regionally important crops, and thus have become more homogeneous globally.<sup id="cite_ref-Khoury_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khoury-94">[94]</a></sup> The differences between the foods eaten in different countries were reduced by 68% between 1961 and 2009. The modern "global standard"<sup id="cite_ref-Khoury_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khoury-94">[94]</a></sup> diet contains an increasingly large percentage of a relatively small number of major staple commodity crops, which have increased substantially in the share of the total food energy (calories), protein, fat, and food weight that they provide to the world's human population, including <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">soybean</a> (by +284%<sup id="cite_ref-Kinver_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinver-95">[95]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a> (by +173%<sup id="cite_ref-Kinver_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinver-95">[95]</a></sup>), and <a href="/wiki/Sunflower" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunflower">sunflower</a> (by +246%<sup id="cite_ref-Kinver_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinver-95">[95]</a></sup>). Whereas nations used to consume greater proportions of locally or regionally important crops, wheat has become a staple in over 97% of countries, with the other global staples showing similar dominance worldwide. Other crops have declined sharply over the same period, including <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">rye</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yam_(vegetable)" title="Yam (vegetable)">yam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweet_potato" title="Sweet potato">sweet potato</a> (by -45%<sup id="cite_ref-Kinver_95-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinver-95">[95]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Cassava" title="Cassava">cassava</a> (by -38%<sup id="cite_ref-Kinver_95-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinver-95">[95]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Coconut" title="Coconut">coconut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a> (by -52%<sup id="cite_ref-Kinver_95-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinver-95">[95]</a></sup>) and <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millets</a> (by -45%<sup id="cite_ref-Kinver_95-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinver-95">[95]</a></sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-Khoury_94-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khoury-94">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kinver_95-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinver-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SciAm_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SciAm-96">[96]</a></sup> Such crop diversity change in the human diet is associated with mixed effects on food security, improving under-nutrition in some regions but contributing to the diet-related diseases caused by over-consumption of macronutrients.<sup id="cite_ref-Khoury_94-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khoury-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Price_setting">Price setting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Price setting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On April 30, 2008, Thailand, one of the world's biggest rice exporters, announced the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Rice_Exporting_Countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Organisation of Rice Exporting Countries">Organisation of Rice Exporting Countries</a> with the potential to develop into a price-fixing cartel for rice. It is a project to organize 21 rice exporting countries to create a homonymous organisation to control the price of rice. The group is mainly made up of Thailand, Vietnam, Cambodia, Laos and Myanmar. The organization attempts to serve the purpose of making a "contribution to ensuring food stability, not just in an individual country but also to address food shortages in the region and the world". However, it is still questionable whether this organization will serve its role as an effective rice price fixing cartel, that is similar to OPEC's mechanism for managing petroleum. Economic analysts and traders said the proposal would go nowhere because of the inability of governments to cooperate with each other and control farmers' output. Moreover, countries that are involved expressed their concern, that this could only worsen the food security.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_use_change">Land use change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Land use change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>China needs not less than 120 million hectares of arable land for its food security. China has recently reported a surplus of 15 million hectares. On the other side of the coin, some 4 million hectares of conversion to urban use and 3 million hectares of contaminated land have been reported as well.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Furthermore, a survey found that 2.5% of China's arable land is too contaminated to grow food without harm.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> In Europe, the conversion of agricultural soil implied a net loss of potential. But the rapid loss in the area of arable soils appears to be economically meaningless because EU is perceived to be dependent on internal food supply anymore. During the period 2000-2006 the European Union lost 0.27% of its cropland and 0.26% of its crop productive potential. The loss of agricultural land during the same time was the highest in the Netherlands, which lost 1.57% of its crop production potential within six years. The figures are quite alarming for Cyprus (0.84%), Ireland (0.77%) and Spain (0.49%) as well.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> In Italy, in the Emilia-Romagna plain (ERP), the conversion of 15,000 ha of agricultural soil (period 2003-2008) implied a net loss of 109,000 Mg per year of wheat, which accounts for the calories needed by 14% of ERP population (425,000 people). Such a loss in wheat production is just 0.02% of gross domestic product (GDP) of the Emilia-Romagna region which is actually a minor effect in financial terms. Additionally, the income from the new land use is often much higher than the one guaranteed by agriculture, as in the case of urbanisation or extraction of raw materials.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_catastrophic_risks">Global catastrophic risks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Global catastrophic risks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Global_catastrophic_risk" title="Global catastrophic risk">Global catastrophic risk</a></div>
<p>As anthropogenic greenhouse gas emissions reduce the stability of the global climate,<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Abrupt_climate_change" title="Abrupt climate change">abrupt climate change</a> could become more intense.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The impact of an <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a> larger than about 1&#160;km diameter has the potential to block the sun globally,<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-107">[107]</a></sup> causing <a href="/wiki/Impact_winter" title="Impact winter">impact winter</a>. Particles in the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a> would quickly rain out, but particles in the <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfate</a>, could remain there for years.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-107">[107]</a></sup> Similarly, a <a href="/wiki/Supervolcano" title="Supervolcano">supervolcanic</a> eruption would reduce the potential of agricultural production from solar photosynthesis, causing <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_winter" title="Volcanic winter">volcanic winter</a>. The Toba super volcanic eruption approximately 70,000 years ago may have nearly caused the extinction of humans<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-107">[107]</a></sup> (see <a href="/wiki/Toba_catastrophe_theory" title="Toba catastrophe theory">Toba catastrophe theory</a>). Again, primarily sulfate particles could block the sun for years. Solar blocking is not limited to natural causes as <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_winter" title="Nuclear winter">nuclear winter</a> is also possible, which refers to the scenario involving widespread nuclear war and burning of cities that release soot into the stratosphere that would stay there for about 10 years.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The high stratospheric temperatures produced by soot absorbing solar radiation would create near-global ozone hole conditions even for a regional nuclear conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Children_and_food_security">Children and food security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Children and food security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bengal_famine_1943.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Bengal_famine_1943.png/220px-Bengal_famine_1943.png" width="220" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Bengal_famine_1943.png/330px-Bengal_famine_1943.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/87/Bengal_famine_1943.png 2x" data-file-width="396" data-file-height="359" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bengal_famine_1943.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bengali famine, 1943. The Japanese conquest of Burma cut off India's main supply of <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> imports.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On April 29, 2008, a <a href="/wiki/UNICEF_UK" title="UNICEF UK">UNICEF UK</a> report found that the world's poorest and most vulnerable children are being hit the hardest by <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>. The report, "Our Climate, Our Children, Our Responsibility: The Implications of Climate Change for the World's Children", says that access to clean water and food supplies will become more difficult, particularly in Africa and Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>By way of comparison, in one of the largest food producing countries in the world, the United States, approximately one out of six people are "food insecure", including 17 million children, according to the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of Agriculture">U.S. Department of Agriculture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> A 2012 study in the <i>Journal of Applied Research on Children</i> found that rates of food security varied significantly by race, class and education. In both kindergarten and third grade, 8% of the children were classified as food insecure, but only 5% of white children were food insecure, while 12% and 15% of black and Hispanic children were food insecure, respectively. In third grade, 13% of black and 11% of Hispanic children are food insecure compared to 5% of white children.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_and_food_security">Gender and food security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Gender and food security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gender_and_food_security" title="Gender and food security">Gender and food security</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2DU_Kenya_86_(5367322642).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/2DU_Kenya_86_%285367322642%29.jpg/220px-2DU_Kenya_86_%285367322642%29.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/2DU_Kenya_86_%285367322642%29.jpg/330px-2DU_Kenya_86_%285367322642%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/2DU_Kenya_86_%285367322642%29.jpg/440px-2DU_Kenya_86_%285367322642%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2DU_Kenya_86_(5367322642).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Kenyan woman farmer at work in the <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kenya" title="Mount Kenya">Mount Kenya</a> region</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gender_inequality" title="Gender inequality">Gender inequality</a> both leads to and is a result of food insecurity. According to estimates women and girls make up 60% of the world's chronically hungry and little progress has been made in ensuring the equal <a href="/wiki/Right_to_food" title="Right to food">right to food</a> for women enshrined in the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Elimination_of_All_Forms_of_Discrimination_against_Women" class="mw-redirect" title="Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination against Women">Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination against Women</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-food_conference_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-food_conference-116">[116]</a></sup> Women face <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">discrimination</a> both in education and employment opportunities and within the household, where their bargaining power is lower. On the other hand, gender equality is described as instrumental to ending <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hunger" title="Hunger">hunger</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ADB_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADB-117">[117]</a></sup> Women tend to be responsible for food preparation and childcare within the family and are more likely to spend their income on food and their children's needs.<sup id="cite_ref-Agriculture_Sourcebook_2009_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Agriculture_Sourcebook_2009-118">[118]</a></sup> Women also play an important role in food production, processing, distribution and marketing. They often work as unpaid family workers, are involved in subsistence farming and represent about 43% of the agricultural labor force in developing countries, varying from 20% in Latin America to 50% in Eastern and Southeastern Asia and Sub-Saharan Africa. However, women face discrimination in access to land, credit, technologies, finance and other services. Empirical studies suggest that if women had the same access to productive resources as men, women could boost their yields by 20–30%; raising the overall agricultural output in developing countries by 2.5 to 4%. While those are rough estimates, the significant benefit of closing the gender gap on agricultural productivity cannot be denied.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The gendered aspects of food security are visible along the four pillars of food security: availability, access, utilization and stability, as defined by the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>The number of people affected by hunger is extremely high, with enormous effects on women and girls.<sup id="cite_ref-Bridge_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bridge-121">[121]</a></sup> Making this trend disappear "must be a top priority for governments and international institutions".<sup id="cite_ref-Bridge_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bridge-121">[121]</a></sup> Actions governments take must take into consideration that food insecurity is an issue regarding "equality, rights and social justice".<sup id="cite_ref-Bridge_121-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bridge-121">[121]</a></sup> "Food and nutrition insecurity is a political and economic phenomenon fuelled by inequitable global and national processes".<sup id="cite_ref-Bridge_121-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bridge-121">[121]</a></sup> Factors like capitalism, exploration of Indigenous lands all contribute to food insecurity for minorities and the people who are the most oppressed in various countries (women being one of these oppressed groups). To emphasis, "food and nutrition insecurity is a gender justice issue".<sup id="cite_ref-Bridge_121-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bridge-121">[121]</a></sup> The facts that women and girls are the most oppressed by "the inequitable global economic processes that govern food systems and by global trends such as climate change", shows how institutions continue to place women in positions of disadvantage and impoverishment to make money and thrive on capitalizing the food system.<sup id="cite_ref-Bridge_121-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bridge-121">[121]</a></sup> When the government withholds food by raising its prices to amounts only privileged people can afford, they both benefit and are able to control the "lower-class"/ marginalized people via the food market. An interesting fact is that "despite rapid economic growth in India, thousands of women and girls still lack food and nutrition security as a direct result of their lower status compared with men and boys".<sup id="cite_ref-Bridge_121-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bridge-121">[121]</a></sup> "Such inequalities are compounded by women and girls' often limited access to productive resources, education and decision-making, by the 'normalised' burden of unpaid work – including care work – and by the endemic problems of gender-based violence (GBV), HIV and AIDS".<sup id="cite_ref-Bridge_121-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bridge-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_genetically_modified_.28GM.29_crops">Use of genetically modified (GM) crops</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Use of genetically modified (GM) crops">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>One of the most up-and-coming techniques to ensuring global food security is the use of <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">genetically modified (GM) crops</a>. The genome of these crops can be altered to address one or more aspects of the plant that may be preventing it from being grown in various regions under certain conditions. Many of these alterations can address the challenges that were previously mentioned above, including the water crisis, land degradation, and the ever-changing climate.</p>
<p>In agriculture and <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a> popularized the use of conventional <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridization</a> to increase yield by creating "<a href="/wiki/High-yielding_varieties" class="mw-redirect" title="High-yielding varieties">high-yielding varieties</a>". Often the handful of hybridized breeds originated in developed countries and were further hybridized with local varieties in the rest of the developing world to create high yield strains resistant to local climate and diseases.</p>
<p>The area sown to genetically engineered crops in developing countries is rapidly catching up with the area sown in industrial nations. According to the International Service for the Acquisition of Agri-biotech Applications (ISAAA), GM crops were grown by approximately 8.5 million farmers in 21 countries in 2005; up from 8.25 million farmers in 17 countries in 2004. However, the ISAAA is funded by organisations including prominent agricultural biotechnology corporations, such as Monsanto and Bayer,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and there have been several challenges made to the accuracy of ISAAA's global figures.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition_to_GM_crops">Opposition to GM crops</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Opposition to GM crops">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Respected scientists question the safety of biotechnology as a panacea; agroecologists <a href="/wiki/Miguel_Altieri" title="Miguel Altieri">Miguel Altieri</a> and Peter Rosset have enumerated ten reasons<sup id="cite_ref-altros_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-altros-122">[122]</a></sup> why biotechnology will not ensure food security, protect the environment, or reduce poverty. Reasons include:</p>
<ol>
<li>There is no relationship between the prevalence of hunger in a given country and its population</li>
<li>Most innovations in agricultural biotechnology have been profit-driven rather than need-driven</li>
<li>Ecological theory predicts that the large-scale landscape homogenization with transgenic crops will exacerbate the ecological problems already associated with monoculture agriculture</li>
<li>And, that much of the needed food can be produced by small farmers located throughout the world using existing agroecological technologies.</li>
</ol>
<p>Based on evidence from previous attempts, there is a likely lack of transferability of one type of GM crop from one region to another. For example, modified crops that have proven successful in Asia from the Green Revolution have failed when tried in regions of Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> More research must be done regarding the specific requirements of growing a specific crop in a specific region.</p>
<p>There is also a drastic lack of education given to governments, farmers, and the community about the science behind GM crops, as well as suitable growing practices. In most relief programs, farmers are given seeds with little explanation and little attention is paid to the resources available to them or even laws that prohibit them from distributing produce. Governments are often not advised on the economic and health implications that come with growing GM crops, and are then left to make judgments on their own. Because they have so little information regarding these crops, they usually shy away from allowing them or do not take the time and effort required to regulate their use. Members of the community that will then consume the produce from these crops are also left in the dark about what these modifications mean and are often scared off by their 'unnatural' origins. This has resulted in failure to properly grow crops as well as strong opposition to the unknown practices.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>A study published in June 2016 evaluated the status of the implementation of <a href="/wiki/Golden_rice" title="Golden rice">Golden Rice</a>, which was first developed in the 1990s to produce higher levels of Vitamin A than its non-GMO counterparts. This strain of rice was designed so that malnourished women and children in third world countries who were more susceptible to deficiencies could easily improve their Vitamin A intake levels and prevent blindness, which is a common result. Golden Rice production was centralized to the Philippines, yet there have been many hurdles to jump in order to get production moving. The study showed that the project is far behind schedule and is not living up to its expectations. Although research on Golden Rice still continues, the country has moved forward with other non-GMO initiatives to address the Vitamin A deficiency problem which is so prevasive in that region.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many anti-GMO activists argue that the use of GM crops decreases <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> amongst plants. Livestock biodiversity is also threatened by the modernization of agriculture and the focus on more productive major breeds. Therefore, efforts have been made by governments and non-governmental organizations to conserve livestock biodiversity through strategies such as <a href="/wiki/Cryoconservation_of_animal_genetic_resources" title="Cryoconservation of animal genetic resources">Cryoconservation of animal genetic resources</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Support_of_GM_crops">Support of GM crops</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Support of GM crops">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many GM crop success stories exist, primarily in developed nations like the USA, China, and various countries in Europe. Common GM crops include cotton, maize, and soybeans, all of which are grown throughout North and South America as well as regions of Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Modified cotton crops, for example, have been altered such that they are resistant to pests, can grown in more extreme heat, cold, or drought, and produce longer, stronger fibers to be used in textile production.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the biggest threats to rice, which is a staple food crop especially in India and other countries within Asia, is <a href="/wiki/Magnaporthe_grisea" title="Magnaporthe grisea">blast</a> disease which is a fungal infection that causes lesions to form on all parts of the plant.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> A genetically engineered strain of rice has been developed so that it is resistant to blast, greatly improving the crop yield of farmers and allowing rice to be more accessible to everyone.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Some other crops have been modified such that they produce higher yields per plant or that they require less land for growing. The latter can be helpful in extreme climates with little arable land and also decreases deforestation, as fewer trees need to be cut down in order to make room for crop fields.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Others yet have been altered such that they do not require the use of insecticides or fungicides. This addresses various health concerns associated with such pesticides and can also work to improve biodiversity within the area in which these crops are grown.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a review of Borlaug's 2000 publication entitled <i>Ending world hunger: the promise of biotechnology and the threat of antiscience zealotry</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> the authors argued that Borlaug's warnings were still true in 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>GM crops are as natural and safe as today's bread wheat, opined Dr. Borlaug, who also reminded agricultural scientists of their moral obligation to stand up to the antiscience crowd and warn policy makers that global food insecurity will not disappear without this new technology and ignoring this reality global food insecurity would make future solutions all the more difficult to achieve.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Rozwadowski and Kagale</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Research conducted by the GMO Risk Assessment and Communication of Evidence (GRACE) program through the EU between 2007 and 2013 focused on many uses of GM crops and evaluated many facets of their effects on human, animal, and environmental health.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Approaches">Approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carrobotte_g1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Carrobotte_g1.jpg/220px-Carrobotte_g1.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Carrobotte_g1.jpg/330px-Carrobotte_g1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Carrobotte_g1.jpg/440px-Carrobotte_g1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1956" data-file-height="1551" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carrobotte_g1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A liquid manure spreader, equipment that is used to increase <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_productivity" title="Agricultural productivity">agricultural productivity</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_the_United_Nations">By the United Nations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: By the United Nations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/UN_Millennium_Development_Goals" class="mw-redirect" title="UN Millennium Development Goals">UN Millennium Development Goals</a> are one of the initiatives aimed at achieving food security in the world. The first Millennium Development Goal states that the UN "is to eradicate extreme hunger and poverty" by 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-IFPRI_2003_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IFPRI_2003-137">[137]</a></sup> Olivier De Schutter, the UN Special Rapporteur on the Right to Food, advocates for a multidimensional approach to food security challenges. This approach emphasizes the physical availability of food; the social,economic and physical access people have to food; and the nutrition, safety and cultural appropriateness or adequacy of food.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="By_the_Food_and_Agriculture_Organization">By the Food and Agriculture Organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: By the Food and Agriculture Organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations stated in <i>The State of Food Insecurity in the World 2003</i> that countries that have reduced hunger often had rapid economic growth, specifically in their <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agricultural sectors</a>. These countries were also characterized as having slower <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a>, lower HIV rates, and higher rankings in the <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> At that time, the FAO considered addressing agriculture and population growth vital to achieving food security. In <i>The State of Food Insecurity in the World 2012</i>, the FAO restated its focus on <a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">economic growth</a> and agricultural growth to achieve food security and added a focus on the poor and on "nutrition-sensitive" growth. For example, economic growth should be used by governments to provide public services to benefit poor and hungry populations. The FAO also cited smallholders, including women, as groups that should be involved in agricultural growth to generate employment for the poor. For economic and agricultural growth to be "nutrition-sensitive", resources should be utilized to improve access to diverse diets for the poor as well as access to a safe <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">water supply</a> and to healthcare.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO.2C_WFP.2C_IFAD_2012_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO.2C_WFP.2C_IFAD_2012-23">[23]</a></sup> The FAO has proposed a "twin track" approach to fight food insecurity that combines sustainable development and short-term hunger relief. Development approaches include investing in rural markets and rural infrastructure.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-5">[5]</a></sup> In general, the FAO proposes the use of public policies and programs that promote long-term economic growth that will benefit the poor. To obtain short-term food security, <a href="/wiki/Vouchers" class="mw-redirect" title="Vouchers">vouchers</a> for seeds, <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a>, or access to services could promote agricultural production. The use of conditional or unconditional food or cash transfers was another approach the FAO noted. Conditional transfers could include <a href="/wiki/School_feeding_in_low-income_countries" title="School feeding in low-income countries">school feeding programs</a>, while unconditional transfers could include general food distribution, <a href="/wiki/Aid" title="Aid">emergency food aid</a> or cash transfers. A third approach is the use of <a href="/wiki/Subsidy" title="Subsidy">subsidies</a> as safety nets to increase the purchasing power of households. The FAO stated that "approaches should be human rights-based, target the poor, promote gender equality, enhance long-term resilience and allow sustainable graduation out of poverty."<sup id="cite_ref-FAO.2C_WFP.2C_IFAD_2012_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO.2C_WFP.2C_IFAD_2012-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The FAO noted that some countries have been successful in fighting food insecurity and decreasing the number of people suffering from undernourishment. Bangladesh is an example of a country that has met the Millennium Development Goal hunger target. The FAO credited growth in agricultural productivity and macroeconomic stability for the rapid economic growth in the 1990s that resulted in an increase in food security. <a href="/wiki/Irrigation_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Irrigation systems">Irrigation systems</a> were established through infrastructure development programs. Two programs, HarvestPlus and the Golden Rice Project, provided <a href="/wiki/Biofortification" title="Biofortification">biofortified crops</a> in order to decrease micronutrient deficiencess.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO.2C_WFP.2C_IFAD_2013_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO.2C_WFP.2C_IFAD_2013-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/World_Food_Day" title="World Food Day">World Food Day</a> was established on October 16, in honor of the date that the FAO was founded in 1945. On this day, the FAO hosts a variety of event at the headquarters in Rome and around the world, as well as seminars with UN officials.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_World_Food_Day_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_World_Food_Day-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="By_the_World_Food_Programme">By the World Food Programme</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: By the World Food Programme">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WalktheWorldLogo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/WalktheWorldLogo.jpg/220px-WalktheWorldLogo.jpg" width="220" height="96" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/WalktheWorldLogo.jpg/330px-WalktheWorldLogo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/WalktheWorldLogo.jpg/440px-WalktheWorldLogo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="529" data-file-height="232" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WalktheWorldLogo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fight Hunger: Walk the World campaign is a United Nations <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Programme" title="World Food Programme">World Food Programme</a> initiative.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Programme" title="World Food Programme">World Food Programme</a> (WFP) is an agency of the United Nations that uses <a href="/wiki/Food_aid" class="mw-redirect" title="Food aid">food aid</a> to promote food security and eradicate hunger and poverty. In particular, the WFP provides food aid to refugees and to others experiencing food emergencies. It also seeks to improve nutrition and quality of life to the most vulnerable populations and promote self-reliance.<sup id="cite_ref-WFP_Mission_Statement_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WFP_Mission_Statement-140">[140]</a></sup> An example of a WFP program is the "Food For Assets" program in which participants work on new infrastructure, or learn new skills, that will increase food security, in exchange for food.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The WFP and the Government of Kenya have partnered in the Food For Assets program in hopes of increasing the resilience of communities to shocks.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_partnerships_to_achieve_food_security_and_end_hunger">Global partnerships to achieve food security and end hunger</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Global partnerships to achieve food security and end hunger">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In April 2012, the <a href="/wiki/Food_Assistance_Convention" title="Food Assistance Convention">Food Assistance Convention</a> was signed, the world's first legally binding international agreement on food aid. The May 2012 <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen_Consensus" title="Copenhagen Consensus">Copenhagen Consensus</a> recommended that efforts to combat hunger and malnutrition should be the first priority for politicians and private sector philanthropists looking to maximize the effectiveness of aid spending. They put this ahead of other priorities, like the fight against <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/AIDS" class="mw-redirect" title="AIDS">AIDS</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>The main global policy to reduce hunger and poverty are the recently approved <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">Sustainable Development Goals</a>. In particular Goal 2: Zero Hunger sets globally agreed targets to end hunger, achieve food security and improved nutrition and promote sustainable agriculture by 2030.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> A number of organizations have formed initiatives with the more ambitious goal to achieve this outcome in only 10 years, by 2025:</p>
<ul>
<li>In 2013 Caritas International started a Caritas-wide initiative aimed at ending systemic hunger by 2025. The One human family, food for all campaign focuses on awareness raising, improving the effect of Caritas programs and advocating the implementation of the Right to Food.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></li>
<li>The partnership <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.compact2025.org">Compact2025</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/IFPRI" class="mw-redirect" title="IFPRI">IFPRI</a> with the involvement of UN organisations, NGOs and private foundations<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> develops and disseminates evidence-based advice to politicians and other decision-makers aimed at ending hunger and undernutrition in the coming 10 years, by 2025.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> It bases its claim that hunger can be ended by 2025 on a report by <a href="/wiki/Shenggen_Fan" title="Shenggen Fan">Shenggen Fan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Polman" title="Paul Polman">Paul Polman</a> that analyzed the experiences from China, Vietnam, Brazil and Thailand and concludes that eliminating hunger and undernutrition was possible by 2025.<sup id="cite_ref-IFPRI2013_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IFPRI2013-148">[148]</a></sup></li>
<li>In June 2015, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bill_%26_Melinda_Gates_Foundation" title="Bill &amp; Melinda Gates Foundation">Bill &amp; Melinda Gates Foundation</a> have launched a partnership to combat undernutrition especially in children. The program will initiatilly be implemented in Bangladesh, Burundi, Ethiopia, Kenya, Laos and Niger and will help these countries to improve information and analysis about nutrition so they can develop effective national nutrition policies.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> of the UN has created a partnership that will act through the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a>'s CAADP framework aiming to end hunger in Africa by 2025. It includes different interventions including support for improved food production, a strengthening of social protection and integration of the Right to Food into national legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_the_United_States_Agency_for_International_Development">By the United States Agency for International Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: By the United States Agency for International Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">United States Agency for International Development</a> (USAID) proposes several key steps to increasing <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_productivity" title="Agricultural productivity">agricultural productivity</a> which is in turn key to increasing rural income and reducing food insecurity.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> They include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Boosting <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_science" title="Agricultural science">agricultural science</a> and technology. Current agricultural yields are insufficient to feed the growing populations. Eventually, the rising agricultural productivity drives economic growth.</li>
<li>Securing property rights and <a href="/wiki/Access_to_finance" title="Access to finance">access to finance</a>.</li>
<li>Enhancing <a href="/wiki/Human_capital" title="Human capital">human capital</a> through education and improved health.</li>
<li>Conflict prevention and resolution mechanisms and democracy and governance based on principles of accountability and transparency in public institutions and the rule of law are basic to reducing vulnerable members of society.</li>
</ul>
<p>Since the 1960s, the U.S. has been implementing a Food Stamp Program (now called the Supplemental Nutrition Assistance Program) to directly target consumers who lack the income to purchase food. According to Tim Josling, a Senior Fellow at the Freeman Spogli Institute for International Studies, <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a>, food stamps or other methods of distribution of purchasing power directly to consumers might fit into the range of international programs under consideration to tackle food insecurity.<sup id="cite_ref-josling_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-josling-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Improving_agricultural_productivity_to_benefit_the_rural_poor">Improving agricultural productivity to benefit the rural poor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Improving agricultural productivity to benefit the rural poor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Africa_Food_Security_15_(10665294293).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Africa_Food_Security_15_%2810665294293%29.jpg/220px-Africa_Food_Security_15_%2810665294293%29.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Africa_Food_Security_15_%2810665294293%29.jpg/330px-Africa_Food_Security_15_%2810665294293%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Africa_Food_Security_15_%2810665294293%29.jpg/440px-Africa_Food_Security_15_%2810665294293%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3274" data-file-height="2177" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Africa_Food_Security_15_(10665294293).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A farmer on the outskirts of <a href="/wiki/Lilongwe" title="Lilongwe">Lilongwe</a> (<a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>) prepares a field for planting.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are strong, direct relationships between agricultural productivity, hunger, poverty, and sustainability. Three-quarters of the world's poor live in rural areas and make their living from agriculture. Hunger and child <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> are greater in these areas than in urban areas. Moreover, the higher the proportion of the rural population that obtains its income solely from subsistence farming (without the benefit of pro-poor technologies and access to markets), the higher the incidence of malnutrition. Therefore, improvements in agricultural productivity aimed at small-scale farmers will benefit the rural poor first. Food and feed crop demand is likely to double in the next 50 years, as the global population approaches nine billion. Growing sufficient food will require people to make changes such as increasing productivity in areas dependent on <a href="/wiki/Rainfed_agriculture" title="Rainfed agriculture">rainfed agriculture</a>; improving <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> fertility management; expanding cropped areas; investing in <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a>; conducting agricultural trade between countries; and reducing gross food demand by influencing diets and reducing post-harvest losses.</p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_Assessment_of_Water_Management_in_Agriculture" title="Comprehensive Assessment of Water Management in Agriculture">Comprehensive Assessment of Water Management in Agriculture</a>, a major study led by the <a href="/wiki/International_Water_Management_Institute" title="International Water Management Institute">International Water Management Institute</a> (IWMI), managing rainwater and soil moisture more effectively, and using supplemental and small-scale irrigation, hold the key to helping the greatest number of poor people. It has called for a new era of water investments and policies for upgrading rainfed agriculture that would go beyond controlling field-level soil and water to bring new freshwater sources through better local management of rainfall and runoff.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Increased agricultural productivity enables farmers to grow more food, which translates into better diets and, under market conditions that offer a level playing field, into higher farm incomes. With more money, farmers are more likely to diversify production and grow higher-value crops, benefiting not only themselves but the economy as a whole."<sup id="cite_ref-IFPRI_2003_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IFPRI_2003-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>Researchers suggest forming an alliance between the emergency food program and <a href="/wiki/Community-supported_agriculture" title="Community-supported agriculture">community-supported agriculture</a>, as some countries' food stamps cannot be used at farmer's markets and places where food is less processed and grown locally.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> The gathering of wild food plants appears to be an efficient alternative method of subsistence in tropical countries, which may play a role in poverty alleviation.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Producing_food_without_agriculture">Producing food without agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Producing food without agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>David Denkenberger and <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Pearce" title="Joshua Pearce">Joshua Pearce</a> have proposed in <a href="/wiki/Feeding_Everyone_No_Matter_What" title="Feeding Everyone No Matter What">Feeding Everyone No Matter What</a> a variety of alternate foods which convert fossil fuels or biomass into food without sunlight to address sunlight-blocking food security scenarios.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> The solution using fossil fuel energy source is natural gas digesting bacteria.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> One example of a biomass alternate food is that <a href="/wiki/Mushroom" title="Mushroom">mushrooms</a> can grow directly on wood without sunlight.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Another example is that <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_biofuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Cellulosic biofuel">cellulosic biofuel</a> production typically already creates sugar as an intermediate product.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Large-scale_food_stockpiling">Large-scale food stockpiling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Large-scale food stockpiling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The minimum annual global wheat storage is approximately 2 months.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> To counteract the severe food security issues caused by <a href="/wiki/Global_catastrophic_risks" class="mw-redirect" title="Global catastrophic risks">global catastrophic risks</a>, years of <a href="/wiki/Food_storage" title="Food storage">food storage</a> has been proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> Though this could ameliorate smaller scale problems like regional conflict and drought, it would exacerbate current food insecurity by raising food prices.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_insurances">Agricultural insurances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Agricultural insurances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Insurance is a financial instrument, which allows exposed individuals to pool resources to spread their risk. They do so by contributing premium to an insurance fund, which will indemnify those who suffer insured loss. This procedure reduces the risk for an individual by spreading his/her risk among the multiple fund contributors. Insurance can be designed to protect many types of individuals and assets against single or multiple perils and buffer insured parties against sudden and dramatic income or asset loss.</p>
<p>Crop insurance is purchased by agricultural producers to protect themselves against either the loss of their crops due to natural disasters. Two type of insurances are available:<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> (1) claim-based insurances, and (2) index-based insurances. In particular in poor countries facing food security problems, index-based insurances offer some interesting advantages: 1) indices can be derived from globally available satellite images that correlate well with what is insured; (2) these indices can be delivered at low cost; and (3) the insurance products open up new markets that are not served by claim-based insurances.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Stay_on_topic" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles"><span title="The material near this tag may contain information that is not relevant to the article's main topic. (November 2015)">relevant?</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Food_security" title="Talk:Food security">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup></p>
<p>An advantage of index-based insurance is that it can potentially be delivered at lower cost. A significant barrier that hinders uptake of claim-based insurance is the high transaction cost for searching for prospective policyholders, negotiating and administering contracts, verifying losses and determining payouts. Index insurance eliminates the loss verification step, thereby mitigating a significant transaction cost. A second advantage of index-based insurance is that, because it pays an indemnity based on the reading of an index rather than individual losses, it eliminates much of the fraud, moral hazard and adverse selection, which are common in classical claim-based insurance. A further advantage of index insurance is that payments based on a standardized and indisputable index also allow for a fast indemnity payment. The indemnity payment could be automated, further reducing transaction costs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Stay_on_topic" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles"><span title="The material near this tag may contain information that is not relevant to the article's main topic. (November 2015)">relevant?</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Food_security" title="Talk:Food security">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Basis risk is a major disadvantage of index-based insurance. It is the situation where an individual experiences a loss without receiving payment or vice versa. Basis risk is a direct result of the strength of the relation between the index that estimates the average loss by the insured group and the loss of insured assets by an individual. The weaker this relation the higher the basis risk. It is obvious that high basis risk undermines the willingness of potential clients to purchase insurance. It thus challenges insurance companies to design insurances such as to minimize basis risk.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Stay_on_topic" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles"><span title="The material near this tag may contain information that is not relevant to the article's main topic. (November 2015)">relevant?</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Food_security" title="Talk:Food security">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup></p>
<p><b>Food Security, Quality through Bees</b></p>
<p>Bees and other pollinating insects are currently improving the food production of 2 billion small farmers worldwide, helping to ensure food security for the world’s population. Research shows that if pollination is managed well on small diverse farms, with all other factors being equal, crop yields can increase by a significant median of 24 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Food_security&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the concept of food security has mostly focused on food calories rather than the quality and <a href="/wiki/Nutrition" title="Nutrition">nutrition</a> of food. The concept of nutrition security evolved over time. In 1995, it has been defined as "adequate nutritional status in terms of protein, energy, vitamins, and minerals for all household members at all times".<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:16</sup></p>
<p>How animal <a href="/wiki/Pollinator" title="Pollinator">pollinators</a> positively affect fruit condition and <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrient</a> content is still being discovered.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
